❓:Try to determine the real number a_{0}, such that the sequence left{a_{n}right} defined by the recurrence formula a_{n+1}=-3 a_{n}+2^{n}(n=0,1, cdots) is strictly increasing, i.e., for n geqslant 0 we have a_{n}<a_{n+1}.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. I need to find the real number ( a_0 ) such that the sequence ( {a_n} ) defined by the recurrence ( a_{n+1} = -3a_n + 2^n ) is strictly increasing. That means for every ( n geq 0 ), we must have ( a_n < a_{n+1} ). Hmm, so starting from ( a_0 ), each subsequent term has to be larger than the previous one. First, let me recall what a strictly increasing sequence means. For all ( n ), ( a_{n+1} > a_n ). Given the recurrence relation, I can substitute that into the inequality. So, ( a_{n+1} = -3a_n + 2^n > a_n ). Let me write that down:( -3a_n + 2^n > a_n )Let me solve this inequality for ( a_n ). Adding ( 3a_n ) to both sides gives:( 2^n > 4a_n )Dividing both sides by 4:( a_n < frac{2^n}{4} = frac{2^{n}}{2^2} = 2^{n - 2} )So, this tells me that for each ( n geq 0 ), ( a_n ) must be less than ( 2^{n - 2} ). But this is a condition that must hold for all ( n ), so perhaps I need to find an ( a_0 ) such that every term in the sequence satisfies ( a_n < 2^{n - 2} ). But how do I ensure that? Since the recurrence is linear, maybe I can solve the recurrence relation explicitly. If I can find a general formula for ( a_n ) in terms of ( a_0 ), then I can set up the inequalities ( a_n < a_{n+1} ) for all ( n ), which might give me conditions on ( a_0 ).Let me try solving the recurrence relation. The given recurrence is linear and nonhomogeneous. The general solution should be the sum of the homogeneous solution and a particular solution.First, the homogeneous equation is ( a_{n+1} = -3a_n ). The characteristic equation is ( r = -3 ), so the homogeneous solution is ( a_n^{(h)} = C(-3)^n ), where ( C ) is a constant determined by initial conditions.Now, for the particular solution ( a_n^{(p)} ). The nonhomogeneous term is ( 2^n ). Since the nonhomogeneous term is of the form ( 2^n ), and ( 2 ) is not a root of the characteristic equation (which was ( r = -3 )), we can try a particular solution of the form ( A cdot 2^n ).Let me substitute ( a_n^{(p)} = A cdot 2^n ) into the recurrence equation:( a_{n+1}^{(p)} = -3a_n^{(p)} + 2^n )Left side: ( A cdot 2^{n+1} )Right side: ( -3A cdot 2^n + 2^n )So:( A cdot 2^{n+1} = (-3A + 1) cdot 2^n )Divide both sides by ( 2^n ):( 2A = -3A + 1 )Solving for ( A ):( 2A + 3A = 1 )( 5A = 1 )( A = frac{1}{5} )So, the particular solution is ( a_n^{(p)} = frac{1}{5} cdot 2^n ).Therefore, the general solution is:( a_n = a_n^{(h)} + a_n^{(p)} = C(-3)^n + frac{1}{5}2^n )Now, we can use the initial condition ( a_0 ) to find ( C ). Now, the problem states that the sequence must be strictly increasing for all ( n geq 0 ). The homogeneous solution is found by solving ( c_{n+1} = -3c_n ), which gives ( c_n = (-3)^n ). 🔑:15. It is known that a_{1}=-3 a_{0}+1, a_{2}=3^{2} a_{0}-3+2, a_{3}=-3^{3} a_{0}+3^{3}-3 times 2+2^{2}, a_{4}=3^{4} a_{0}-3^{2}+3^{2} times 2-3 times 2^{2}+2^{3}, from which we can expect that in general, a_{n}=(-3)^{n} a_{0}+(-1)^{n-1}left(3^{n 1}-3^{n-2} times 2+cdots cdots 3 times 2^{n 2}right)+2^{n-1}. This can be easily proven by induction (omitted here). The above formula can be rewritten as:a_{n}=left(a_{0}-frac{1}{5}right)(-3)^{n}+frac{2^{n}}{5},which can be further transformed to:a_{n}=(-3)^{n}left[left(a_{0}-frac{1}{5}right)+left(-frac{2}{3}right)^{n} times frac{1}{5}right]Note: When n is sufficiently large, left(frac{2}{3}right)^{n} tends to 0. Therefore, if a_{0}-frac{1}{5} neq 0, the term in the brackets will be close to a_{0}-frac{1}{5}. Since (-3)^{n} (n=0,1,2, cdots) alternates between positive and negative values, the sequence {a_{n}} cannot be strictly increasing. Therefore, a_{0}-frac{1}{5}=0, i.e., a_{n}=frac{1}{5}. In this case, a_{n}=frac{2^{n}}{5}, which is strictly increasing.The general term formula for a_{n} can also be derived as follows: Rewrite the recurrence relation a_{n+1}=-3 a_{n}+2^{n} as left(a_{n+1}-frac{2^{n+1}}{5}right)=-3left(a_{n}-frac{2^{n}}{5}right). Let b_{n}=a_{n}-frac{2^{n}}{5}(n geqslant 0), then the above equation becomes b_{n+1}=-3 b_{n}, so b_{n}=(-3)^{n} b_{0}, from which we get a_{n}=left(a_{0}-frac{1}{5}right)(-3)^{n}+frac{2^{n}}{5}(n geqslant 0).

❓:7.4. Four pirates divided a treasure of 100 coins. It is known that among them, there are exactly two liars (who always lie) and exactly two knights (who always tell the truth).They said:First pirate: “We divided the coins equally.”Second pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got at least 15 coins.”Third pirate: “Each person’s number of coins is divisible by 5.”Fourth pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got no more than 35 coins.”What is the maximum number of coins that could have gone to one pirate?

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's tackle this pirate coin problem. Hmm, there are four pirates, two liars, and two knights. Each made a statement about how the 100 coins were divided. We need to figure out the maximum number of coins one pirate could have received. Alright, let's break down each pirate's statement and see what we can deduce.First, let's list out the statements again:1. First pirate: “We divided the coins equally.”2. Second pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got at least 15 coins.”3. Third pirate: “Each person’s number of coins is divisible by 5.”4. Fourth pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got no more than 35 coins.”Since there are two liars and two knights, exactly two of these statements are true, and two are false. Our goal is to determine the maximum number of coins any one pirate could have, given these constraints.Let me start by analyzing each statement and what it implies if the pirate is a knight (truth-teller) or a liar.First pirate's statement: If true, then each pirate got 25 coins (since 100 divided by 4 is 25). First pirate: “We divided the coins equally.”2. Second pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got at least 15 coins.”3. Third pirate: “Each person’s number of coins is divisible by 5.”4. Fourth pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got no more than 35 coins.”Key steps to determine the maximum number of coins:1. Analyzing Statements: - If the first pirate's statement were true, each pirate would have 25 coins, but this contradicts the second pirate's statement. - If the second and third pirates are knights, their statements imply distinct numbers of coins each at least 15 and divisible by 5. The fourth pirate's statement would then be false, allowing a number greater than 35. - Testing combinations of knights and liars, we find that if the second and third pirates are knights (truth-tellers), their statements require distinct multiples of 5 starting from 15. 2. Possible Distribution: - The smallest distinct multiples of 15, 20, 25, and 30 sum to 90, which is 10 short. Adjusting to 15, 20, 25, and 40 sums to 100. - This distribution satisfies the second pirate's statement (distinct and ≥15) and the third pirate's statement (divisible by 5). The fourth pirate's statement is false (40 > 35), making the fourth pirate a liar. The first pirate's statement is also false, confirming two liars and two knights.3. Verification: - The distribution 15, 20, 25, 40 meets all conditions: sum to 100, distinct numbers, each ≥15, divisible by 5, and one number exceeding 35. This results in two knights (second and third pirates) and two liars (first and fourth pirates).Thus, the maximum number of coins that could have gone to one pirate is (boxed{40}).,<think>Alright, let's tackle this pirate coin problem. So, there are four pirates who divided 100 coins. Two are liars (always lie) and two are knights (always tell the truth). Each pirate made a statement, and we need to figure out the maximum number of coins one pirate could have, given these statements. The pirates' statements are:1. First pirate: “We divided the coins equally.”2. Second pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got at least 15 coins.”3. Third pirate: “Each person’s number of coins is divisible by 5.”4. Fourth pirate: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got no more than 35 coins.”Our goal is to find the maximum number of coins any one pirate could have. Let's break this down step by step.First, let's note that since there are two liars and two knights, exactly two of these statements are false and two are true. So, we need to determine which two statements are lies and which two are truths, and then see what coin distributions are possible under those scenarios, particularly looking for the one that allows a pirate to have the maximum number of coins.Let me analyze each statement one by one and see their implications.First Pirate's Statement: “We divided the coins equally.”If this is true, then each pirate has 25 coins (since 100 divided by 4 is 25). But if this is the case, then all pirates have the same number of coins, which would contradict the second pirate's statement if it were true (since the second pirate says everyone has different numbers). Similarly, if the first pirate is telling the truth, then the third pirate's statement would also be true because 25 is divisible by 5. But the fourth pirate's statement would also be true because 25 is less than 35. However, since there are only two knights, the first pirate can't be a knight because that would make three statements true (first, third, fourth), which is more than allowed. Therefore, the first pirate is likely a liar. So, the coins were not divided equally. Let's tentatively mark the first pirate as a liar.Second Pirate's Statement: “Everyone has a different number of coins, but each got at least 15 coins.”If this is true, then all four pirates have distinct amounts, each at least 15. The minimum total in this case would be 15 + 16 + 17 + 18 = 66, which is much less than 100. So, there's a lot of room for higher numbers. However, if the second pirate is a knight, then the other knight's statement must also be compatible. If the second pirate is a liar, then either not everyone has a different number, or at least one pirate has less than 15 coins.Third Pirate's Statement: “Each person’s number of coins is divisible by 5.”If this is true, then all four pirates have amounts like 5, 10, 15, etc., adding up to 100. 🔑:Answer: 40 coinsSolution. Note that the first and fourth pirates could not have told the truth simultaneously.If the first pirate is a knight, then everyone received 25 coins (such a situation is possible). In this case, the maximum number is 25.If the fourth pirate is a knight, then each received no more than 35 coins. And in this case, the maximum number of coins is no more than 35.If both the first and fourth pirates are liars, then the second and third pirates told the truth. Then any three pirates received no less than 15+20+25=60 coins, so the fourth pirate received no more than 40. Such a situation is possible if the pirates received 15, 20, 35, and 40 coins. In this case, the maximum number is 40 (and the answer to the problem is 40).Comment. 🔑:-、1.C.Since 4 x^{2} y and 8 x^{3} y are not like terms, therefore, the sum of 4 x^{2} y and m x^{3-n^{2}} y is 0 or the sum of m x^{3-n^{2}} y and 8 x^{3} y is 0.Thus, we have left{begin{array}{l}m=-4, 3-n^{2}=2end{array}right. or left{begin{array}{l}m=-8, 3-n^{2}=3 .end{array}right.Solving, we get left{begin{array}{l}m=-4, n= pm 1end{array}right. or left{begin{array}{l}m=-8, n=0 .end{array}right.Hence, m+n=-5,-3 or -8.

The length of side (AC) is (sqrt{6} + sqrt{2}). The distances from the incenter of triangle (DEF) to points (A) and (C) are 2 and (2sqrt{2}), respectively. We need to find the radius of the circumscribed circle around triangle (DEF).1. Setting up Coordinates: - Place (A) at ((0, 0)) and (C) at ((sqrt{6} + sqrt{2}, 0)). - The incenter (I) of (DEF) is given to be ((sqrt{2}, sqrt{2})).2. Verification of Coordinates: - The distance from (I) to (A) is (sqrt{(sqrt{2})^2 + (sqrt{2})^2} = 2). - The distance from (I) to (C) is (sqrt{(sqrt{6} + sqrt{2} - sqrt{2})^2 + (sqrt{2})^2} = 2sqrt{2}).3. Coordinates of (B): - Assume (B) is at ((sqrt{2}, sqrt{2})) to simplify calculations, leading to the coordinates of (D), (E), and (F).4. Calculating Circumradius of (ABC): - Sides of (ABC): - (AB = 2) - (BC = 2sqrt{2}) - (AC = sqrt{6} + sqrt{2}) - Area (K) of (ABC): - (K = frac{1}{2} times (sqrt{6} + sqrt{2}) times sqrt{2} = sqrt{3} + 1) - Circumradius (R) of (ABC): - (R = frac{abc}{4K} = frac{2 times 2sqrt{2} times (sqrt{6} + sqrt{2})}{4(sqrt{3} + 1)} = 2)5. Circumradius of (DEF): - The circumradius of the orthic triangle (DEF) is half the circumradius of (ABC). - Therefore, the circumradius of (DEF) is (frac{R}{2} = frac{2}{2} = 1).The radius of the circumscribed circle around triangle (DEF) is (boxed{1}).]

🔑:Solution. quad A D, B E, C F are the altitudes of triangle A B C, D A, E B, F C are the angle bisectors of angles D, E, F of triangle D E F, O is the point of intersection of the altitudes of triangle A B C, which is also the center of the inscribed circle of triangle D E F. Thus, A O=2, C O=2 sqrt{2}. Let O E=x, A E=y. Then we arrive at the system left{begin{array}{c}x^{2}+y^{2}=4, (sqrt{2}+sqrt{6}-y)^{2}+x^{2}=8 .end{array}right. Solving the system, we get y=sqrt{2}, x=sqrt{2}. Then quad angle D A C=angle B C A=45^{circ}, quad B C=2 sqrt{3},![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2024_05_06_d66465e084edd0e7e96fg-11.jpg?height=414&width=620&top_left_y=564&top_left_x=1369)angle F C A=operatorname{arctg} frac{1}{sqrt{3}}=30^{circ}, quad angle A B E=30^{circ}, quad A B=2 sqrt{2}. angle D F E=90^{circ}, D E=A B cos 45^{circ}=2 . quad R_{o n .}=D E / 2=1.## Answer: 1.
